Rev. 132, 174; K Ziolkowski, ‘Computer Network Operations and the Law of Armed Conflict’ (2010) 49 Military Law &amp; Law of War Review 47, 69.\n¹⁷⁰. E Nakashima, ‘War game reveals U.S. lacks cyber-crisis skills’ (*The Washington Post*, 17 February 2010), available at <http: 2010-02-17=\"\"36782381_1_cyber=\"\"articles.washingtonpost.co=\"\"news=\"\"wwws=\"\"> (accessed 6 April 2021).\n¹⁷¹. *Nicaragua* (n 13) para 249.\n¹⁷². ILC, Responsibility of States for Internationally Wrongful Acts with commentaries UN Doc A/56/10 (2001) (State Responsibility Commentary), Art 50(1)(a).\n© 2021 The Author Journal compilation © 2021 Edward Elgar Publishing Ltd</http:> The Military Law and the Law of War Review, Vol. 59 No. 1 doctrine and also recognizes the vital part that those institutions play in the global economy.\n# 3 CYBER OPERATIONS NOT REACHING THE THRESHOLD OF ARTICLE 2(4) UN CHARTER ## 3.1 Cyber operations amounting to an unlawful intervention to the internal affairs of a state In a case when a cyber attack is not disruptive enough or if it targets non-critical infrastructures and does not amount to a violation of Article 2(4), this does not render it legal, according to international law. If such an attack is attributed to a state, it can amount to a violation of the principle of non-intervention in the internal affairs of another state.^[173]^ The customary principle^[174]^ of non-intervention derives primarily from the international law notions of sovereignty and territory.^[175]^ Non-intervention not only permits states to operate free from outside interference by other states, but also reinforces the notion of sovereign equality among states.^[176]^ The common denominator of all unlawful interventions, and the way to differentiate it by the violation of sovereignty,^[177]^ is, according to the ICJ, the element of coercion.^[178]^ Thus, to qualify as an unlawful intervention, interference must be forcible, dictatorial or otherwise coercive and in effect depriving the state of control over the matter in question.^[179]^ What distinguishes intervention from a violation of Article 2(4) is that it can occur 'with or without armed force'.^[180]^ The principle of non-intervention has been incorporated in a multitude of international agreements.^[181]^ In addition, it appears in a series of UN Documents. The 1965 UN General Assembly Declaration on the Inadmissibility of Intervention in the Domestic Affairs of States and the Protection of their Independence and Sovereignty,^[182]^ the 1970 Declaration on Friendly Relations,^[183]^ the 1976 Declaration on Non-interference^[184]^ and the 1981 General Assembly Declaration on the Inadmissibility of Intervention and Interference in the Internal Affairs of States^[185]^ all condemn all © 2021 The Author Journal compilation © 2021 Edward Elgar Publishing Ltd The prohibition of the threat or use of force in cyberspace 109 forms of intervention and they use similarly broad language, able to include cyber attacks below the level of the use of force.¹⁸⁶ Moreover, state practice seems to agree with this particular way of thinking. In 1989 a bilateral treaty was signed between the US and the Soviet Union on the prevention of dangerous military activities which *inter alia* prohibits ‘interfering with command and control networks in a manner which could cause harm to personnel or damage to equipment of the armed forces of the other Party’.¹⁸⁷ Article I(9) of the treaty in particular, defines ‘interference with command and control networks’ as ‘actions that hamper, interrupt or limit the operation of the signals and information transmission means and systems providing for the control of personnel and equipment of the armed forces of a Party’.¹⁸⁸ A cyber attack can also amount to unlawful intervention in cases other than low level disruption. Defacing websites in order to cause civil strife in a state, incitement to violence through cyber means¹⁸⁹ or sending emails to voters before elections in another state to influence them, may be considered prime examples of unlawful intervention.¹⁹⁰ A good example would be the cyber attacks in September 2012 in Azerbaijan, conducted by the ‘Armenian Cyber Army’. Said attacks were supposedly under the ‘direction and control’ of Armenia and were ‘aimed at glorifying terrorists and insulting their victims, as well as at advocating, promoting and inciting ethnically and religiously motivated hatred, discrimination and violence’.¹⁹¹ As it has been observed, if a broadcast ‘is deliberately false and intended to produce dissent or encourage insurgents, the non-intervention principle is likely to be breached’.¹⁹² An issue that must be addressed when discussing intervention in a cyber context, is the issue of propaganda. Propaganda has been defined as a communication ‘to shape attitudes and behaviour … for political purposes’.¹⁹³ To constitute an intervention, propaganda must involve not merely persuasion but rather coercion. To discern coercive propaganda, publicists have asked ‘is the audience’s choice of alternatives severely restricted as a result of the use of the instrument?’¹⁹⁴ It might be said with respect to cyber propaganda that legitimacy depends less on means than ‘upon its outcome, actual or expected’.¹⁹⁵ Acts that amount merely to persuasion remain legitimate.¹⁹⁶ ¹⁸⁶. S Watts, ‘Low-Intensity Cyber Operations and the Principle of Non-Intervention’ in J David Ohlin et al. (eds), *Cyber War: Law and Ethics for Virtual Conflicts* 249, 253.\n¹⁸⁷. Art II(1)(d) of the Agreement on the Prevention of Dangerous Military Activities (1989) 28 International Legal Materials 877.\n¹⁸⁸. Ibid.\n¹⁸⁹. S Watts, ‘Public International Law and Cyber Incitements to Violence’ in AF Bayesky and LR Blank (eds), *Incitement to Terrorism* 24.\n¹⁹⁰. Schmitt et al. (n 76) 326; UNGA Res 60/164 (2 March 2006).\n¹⁹¹. Letter dated 6 September 2012 from the Chargé d’affaires a.i. of the Permanent Mission of Azerbaijan to the United Nations addressed to the Secretary-General (7 September 2012) UN Doc A/66/897–S/2012/687 1.\n¹⁹². Jamnejad and Wood (n 177) 374.\n¹⁹³. B Satyanarayana Murty, *Propaganda and World Public Order* (Yale University Press, 1968); G Lucas, ‘State-Sponsored Hacktivism and the Rise of “Soft” War’ in M Gross and T Meisels (eds), *Soft War, The Ethics of Unarmed Conflict* 82.\n¹⁹⁴. Ibid at 29.\n¹⁹⁵. Ibid at 129.\n¹⁹⁶. Q Wright, ‘Espionage and the Doctrine of Non-Intervention in Internal Affairs’ in Q Wright et al. (eds), *Essays on Espionage* (Ohio State University Press, 1962) 5.\n© 2021 The Author Journal compilation © 2021 Edward Elgar Publishing Ltd The Military Law and the Law of War Review, Vol. 59 No. 1 Thus, a state-sponsored internet campaign attempting to persuade a state to adopt a particular treaty regime would not constitute a violation of the principle of non-intervention. Similarly, a state's offer of incentives or favourable treatment to induce a course of action by another state would not constitute intervention. Only where propaganda could be said to constitute significant support for coercion on a level commensurate with the logistical and financial support recognized by the Nicaragua judgment, could cyber propaganda amount to a violation of the principle of non-intervention.^[197]^ An issue that has brought the principle of non-intervention and its application in the cyber context to the forefront, is the disruption of electoral processes. Both the 2016 US presidential election and the 2017 French presidential election have been victims of extended cyber operations.^[198]^ Specifically, in the case of the US election the governing body of the United States Democratic Party, the Democratic National Committee (DNC) was targeted by a series of cyber attacks resulting in data and email theft^[199]^ and the attack was attributed to the Russian Federation which denied it.^[200]^ In the case of the French Presidential election the party of the now President of France, Emmanuel Macron, was hacked and gigabytes of emails and documents were published online.^[201]^ Both of the aforementioned acts have been discussed to be an intervention in the internal affairs of the state, as they interfere with an issue in the domain reserve of the state, the elections.^[202]^ In the twentieth century the notion of 'intervention' was broadened as a consequence of the increased cooperation among states allowing subtler ways of interference than the use of force.^[203]^ Thus, a further broadening of the notion to include cyber operations is now dictated by the interconnectivity of networks and the reliance of the modern society in computer systems.\nSaid broadening may also be supported by the growing record of state practice. First, states have the tendency not to engage openly in cyber attacks, but to hide their responsibility by camouflaging their attacks through technical means.^[204]^ As Thomas © 2021 The Author Journal compilation © 2021 Edward Elgar Publishing Ltd</https:></www.nytimes.com></www.numerama.com></www.washingtonpost.com> Franck has observed, ‘lying about facts... is the tribute scofflaw governments pay to international legal obligations they violate'. In other words, the fact that states attempt to hide their cyber attack is a possible concern that these attacks may constitute unlawful uses of force. Secondly, when states admit that they have been victims of cyber attack, they and their allies usually denounce and condemn the attacks.\nNevertheless, the opinio juris of states has also evolved to engage the non-intervention principle in the context of cyber operations. Specifically, in the 2015 Report of the Group of Governmental Experts on Developments in the Field of Information and Telecommunications in the Context of International Security, it has been specifically mentioned that the principle of non-intervention must be observed in the use of ICTs by States. Furthermore, in a number of subsequent documents, many individual states have reiterated their opinion that cyber operations may violate the principle of non-intervention.\n### 3.2 A cyber threat of force?\nArticle 2(4) of the UN Charter sets besides the prohibition of the use of force, the prohibition of the use of threat to use force as well. A threat of force, according to Ian Brownlie, is an ‘express or implied promise' to ‘resort to force conditional on non-acceptance of certain demands' of the threatening government. The main element of such a threat of force is the coercive intent of the threat. It has been commented that the importance of those two prohibitions is exactly the same with no differences whatsoever. However, it is a fact that the prohibition of the threat to use force has been examined quite less than the prohibition of the actual use of force. The question that is presented is simple: Can a cyber operation amount to a threat to use force?\n\nThe Military Law and the Law of War Review, Vol. 59 No. 1 A threat to use force may be communicated orally, in communications or documents, included in a treaty, or implied by certain military and political actions.²¹⁴ According to the ICJ on the ‘Legality of the Threat or Use of Nuclear Weapons’ Advisory Opinion, if the envisaged use of force is in itself unlawful, the stated readiness to use it would be an unlawful threat of force.²¹⁵ One can consider it pointless to continue discussing such a specialized form of the threat of force, when the notion itself has been this under researched.²¹⁶ Furthermore, the threat of force is a notion which states hesitate to invoke, as they prefer a situation where actions, which would be considered as threats of force, are not considered unlawful and are tolerated.²¹⁷ Moreover, the analysis of the Tallinn Manual, regarding the threat to use force in a cyber context, appears minimal. Besides mentioning the already known prerequisites of a threat to force, it ponders the questions of evaluation of the capacity to conduct a cyber threat of force, and the scenario of a state threatening to use force, while not meaning to do so.²¹⁸ Nevertheless, instances where states complain about unlawful threats of force are not as rare as one might think. Even in the context of cyberspace, threats of force may be manifested as explicit or they may result implicitly from certain conduct.²¹⁹ For example, the demonstration of force for the purpose of exercising political pressure could amount to a threat of force under the requirements of Article 2(4).²²⁰ A possible example would be cyber warfare simulations and other military exercises.²²¹ Moreover, another common, albeit not much discussed form in which threats of force are expressed in cyberspace, are in the form of *ultimata*. Such threatening declarations have been long considered as violating the rule prohibiting threats of force in international law²²² and it ought to be seriously examined whether this should not be the case in the context of cyberspace. Moreover, recent events have evinced that simple Twitter statements can be interpreted as threats of force, when delivered by the relevant figure.²²³ Ultimately, it is submitted that even though elusive as a concept, the threat of force should not be outright excluded in the cyber context. On the contrary, due to the ease of means that cyberspace offers, it appears that the notion has a greater degree of normative space to evolve than in the traditional kinetic context. Even though the application of international law in cyberspace has been tenuous and not without its obstacles, the fact that a threat of force is communicated through cyberspace should be regarded as exactly this: a threat. The internet is just the form of communication employed, which has nothing to do with the actual message.²²⁴ © 2021 The Author Journal compilation © 2021 Edward Elgar Publishing Ltd The prohibition of the threat or use of force in cyberspace 113 # 4 CONCLUSION The provisions of the UN Charter regarding the use of force between states, seem to also apply to cyber attacks, even though their adoption was decades before the appearance of cyberspace. The flexibility of the provision, as well as the interpretative guidance given by theory and jurisprudence, have contributed to the potential invocation of Article 2(4) of the UN Charter, even in cases of cyber attacks.\nIt has been argued that a cyber attack may be considered as the use of armed force, when its effects are similar to those of a kinetic attack. This occurs in cases when cyber attacks result in loss of life, injury of people or physical damage to property, without imposing any certain threshold, regarding the gravity of the use of force. In addition, cases of severe disruption of essential services or infrastructures, caused by cyber operations, may be considered as uses of force, even though no destruction or material damage to property occurred. The increasing digitalization of today's societies has made it possible to cause considerable harm to states through non-destructive means and a modern interpretation and application of Article 2(4) should have taken this into account.\nIn conclusion, cyber attacks conducted by states, that fail to reach the level of the use of force, may be considered a violation of the principle of non-intervention – if they are considered ‘a manifestation of a policy of force’ and if a coercive intent is recognized in that attempt. Finally, the notion of a cyber threat of force is dismissed, due to the ambiguity of the notion, as well as the reluctance of states to even invoke it.\n© 2021 The Author Journal compilation © 2021 Edward Elgar Publishing Ltd"

A case-by-case approach is supported⁶⁹ on the grounds of the absence of any primary source of international law, or the decision of an international tribunal, as well as the possibility that governments, until such a source is found, are likely to resort to the case-by-case approach individually and collectively.⁷⁰ Finally, it is argued that this model will be more appealing to governments worldwide.⁷¹ Considering the problems an open approach could create, and the possible risks of the creation of double standards, Michael Schmitt introduced a set of factors that influence states in their legal assessments of the use of force in and via cyberspace.⁷² He identified six criteria to serve this purpose: severity, immediacy, directness, invasiveness, measurability (of effects) and presumptive legitimacy.⁷³ Those criteria were revised in 2001 and ‘responsibility’ was added.⁷⁴ Finally, with the release of the Tallinn Manual and their inclusion in it, ‘responsibility’ was separated to ‘military character’ and ‘state involvement’ while the ‘presumptive legitimacy’ became ‘presumptive legality’,⁷⁵ classification that was also used for the revised version of the Manual.⁷⁶ According to Schmitt, these criteria are ‘not legal and merely factors that can be expected to influence states when making sure of force appraisals.’⁷⁷ Schmitt’s criteria could not be of course, without criticism. ‘Directness’ for example cannot be considered as the inherent characteristic of the use of force, as already stated above. The primary effects are those on the computer or on the network that was attacked, the secondary effects on the infrastructure operated by said system and the tertiary effects on the people who were affected by the destruction or incapacitation of infrastructure.87 As Waxman notes, ‘modern society’s heavy reliance on interconnected information systems means that the indirect and secondary effects of cyber attacks may be much more consequential than the direct and immediate ones’.88 In addition, in the Nicaragua Judgement, the ICJ expressly recognized that intervention that uses armed force can occur directly or indirectly.89"

Users should consult their preferred citation format's style manual for proper citation formatting.\n-- Your use of this HeinOnline PDF indicates your acceptance of HeinOnline's Terms and Conditions of the license agreement available at https://heinonline.org/HOL/License\nThe Military Law and the Law of War Review, Vol. 59, No. 1, 2021, pp. 89-113\n# The prohibition of the threat or use of force in cyberspace: novel legal frontier or adaptation of old frameworks?\nTriantafyllos Kouloufakos\nLLM, Attorney at Law\nDefined as ‘a domain characterized by the use of electronics and the electromagnetic spectrum to store, modify, and exchange data via networked systems and associated physical infrastructures’ cyberspace is created by people for the people. However, this is not always the case. Cyber operations conducted by states may amount to the use of force. The opinions, though, regarding the threshold that must be reached differ, and there are diverse theories on when a cyber operation constitutes the use of force. Furthermore, there are cases of operations that despite not reaching the threshold of use, fall under the definition of threat of force, under Article 2(4) of the UN Charter. Finally, cyber operations that fail to reach the threshold may still be contrary to international law, constituting a prohibited intervention in the internal affairs of another state.\nLe cyberespace, défini comme « un domaine caractérisé par l'usage de l'électronique et du spectre électromagnétique pour stocker, modifier et échanger des données via des systèmes en réseaux et les structures physiques qui y sont attachées », est créé par des gens et pour les gens. Ce n'est pourtant pas toujours le cas. Des cyberopérations menées par des États peuvent représenter un emploi de la force. Mais les avis divergent quant au seuil devant être atteint, et les théories pour déterminer quand une cyberopération constitue un emploi de la force sont diverses. En outre, il existe des cas d'opérations qui n'atteignent pas ce seuil, mais qui relèvent pourtant de la définition de menace de la force, au sens de l'article 2(4) de la Charte des Nations unies. Enfin, des cyberopérations n'atteignant pas ce seuil peuvent néanmoins être contraires au droit international, en ce qu'elles constituent une intervention interdite dans les affaires intérieures d'un autre État.\nOmschreven als ‘een domein dat wordt gekenmerkt door het gebruik van elektronica en het elektromagnetische spectrum om gegevens op te slaan, te wijzigen en uit te wisselen via netwerksystemen en bijbehorende fysieke infrastructuren’ komt cyberspace tot stand door en voor mensen. Dat is echter niet altijd het geval. Cyberoperaties geleid door staten kunnen neerkomen op het gebruik van geweld. Over de drempel die bereikt moet worden lopen de meningen echter uiteen, en er zijn verschillende theorieën om te bepalen wanneer bij een cyberoperatie sprake is van gebruik van geweld. Verder zijn er gevallen van operaties die, ook al wordt de drempel van geweldgebruik niet bereikt, onder de definitie van bedreiging met geweld vallen, zoals bedoeld in artikel 2, lid 4, van het VN-Handvest. Ten slotte kunnen cyberoperaties die de drempel niet bereiken, nog steeds in strijd zijn met het internationaal recht en gelijkstaan aan een verboden interventie in de interne aangelegenheden van een andere staat.\n© 2021 The Author\nJournal compilation © 2021 Edward Elgar Publishing Ltd\nThe Lyptatts, 15 Lansdown Road, Cheltenham, Glos GL50 2JA, UK\nand The William Pratt House, 9 Dewey Court, Northampton MA 01060-3815, USA\nThe Military Law and the Law of War Review, Vol. 59 No. 1\nDefinido como ‘un dominio caracterizado por el uso de la electrónica y el espectro electromagnético para almacenar, modificar e intercambiar datos a través de sistemas en red e infraestructuras físicas asociadas’, el ciberespacio es creado por personas para las personas. Sin embargo, no siempre ocurre así. Las operaciones cibernéticas realizadas por los Estados pueden equivaler al uso de la fuerza. No obstante, las opiniones sobre el umbral que se debe alcanzar difieren, existiendo diversas teorías sobre cuándo una ciber-operación constituye uso de la fuerza. Además, existen casos de operaciones que, a pesar de no alcanzar el umbral de uso propiamente dicho, entran sin embargo dentro de la definición de amenaza de uso de la fuerza, según el artículo 2 (4) de la Carta de la ONU. Finalmente, las operaciones cibernéticas que no alcanzan dicho umbral pueden, no obstante, seguir siendo contrarias al Derecho Internacional, constituyendo una intervención prohibida en los asuntos internos de otro Estado.\nDefinito como ‘un dominio caracterizzato dall'uso dell'elettronica e dello spettro elettromagnetico per memorizzare, modificare e scambiare dati tramite sistemi in rete e infrastrutture fisiche associate’ il cyberspazio è creato dalle persone per le persone. Tuttavia, non è sempre così. Le operazioni cyber condotte dagli Stati possono equivalere all'uso della forza. Le opinioni, tuttavia, per quanto riguarda la soglia che deve essere oltrepassata sono diverse e ci sono differenti teorie su quando un'operazione cyber costituisca uso della forza. Inoltre, ci sono casi di operazioni che, pur non raggiungendo la soglia di utilizzo, rientrano nella definizione di minaccia all'uso della forza, ai sensi dell'articolo 2, paragrafo 4, della Carta delle Nazioni Unite. Infine, le operazioni cibernetiche che non raggiungono la soglia possono tuttavia essere contrarie al diritto internazionale, costituendo un intervento vietato negli affari interni di un altro Stato.\nDer Cyberspace, der definiert wird als ‘ein Bereich, gekennzeichnet durch den Gebrauch der Elektronik und des elektromagnetischen Spektrums, um Daten über vernetzte Systeme und dazugehörige physische Infrastrukturen zu speichern, zu ändern und auszutauschen’, wird von Menschen für Menschen geschaffen. Dies ist allerdings nicht immer der Fall. Cyberoperationen, die von Staaten durchgeführt werden, können der Anwendung von Gewalt gleichkommen. Allerdings gehen die Meinungen über die zu erreichende Schwelle auseinander, und es gibt verschiedene Theorien zur Frage, wann eine Cyberoperation die Anwendung von Gewalt ausmacht. Außerdem gibt es Fälle von Operationen, die als Androhung von Gewalt im Sinne von Artikel 2(4) der Charta der Vereinten Nationen bezeichnet werden können, und dies obwohl die Schwelle der Gewaltanwendung nicht erreicht wird. Schließlich können Cyberoperationen, die die Schwelle nicht erreichen, trotzdem gegen das Völkerrecht verstoßen, und somit ein verbotenes Eingreifen in die inneren Angelegenheiten eines anderen Staates darstellen.\nKeywords: cyber operations, cyber attacks, use of force, threat of force, non-intervention, cyberspace, Article 2(4)\n# 1 INTRODUCTION\nHic sunt Leones. ‘Here be lions.’ This is what cartographers from the Roman times to the Middle Ages, used on maps to denote unexplored territories, implying the existence of the\n© 2021 The Author\nJournal compilation © 2021 Edward Elgar Publishing Ltd\nunfamiliar perils.^{1} If cyberspace^{2} were a real location appearing on the maps and not just a virtual domain, we would probably read that expression over it.\nModern societies have become increasingly dependent on computers, computer systems, and networks, with vital services now relying on the internet. Over the course of the last three decades, cyberspace has been ‘woven into the fabric of daily life'^{3} and now permeates all aspects of modern society. Millions of people, organizations, governmental and public institutions, corporations and businesses use cyberspace daily to communicate, gather and exchange information, bank, shop, do business, distribute energy, govern and exert influence. At the end of 2017, there were more than four billion internet users around the world (or almost half of the world's total population of 7.65 billion) -- with 2.023 billion users in Asia, 704.8 million in Europe, 437 million in Latin America, 345.6 million in North America, 453.3 million in Africa, 164.0 million in the Middle East, and 28.4 million in Oceania.^{4}\nDigitalization, however, is a double-edged sword: as the US Deputy Secretary of Defence has emphasized, ‘[i]n the 21st Century, bits and bytes can be as threatening as bullets and bombs'.^{5} A variety of new offensive weapons have been placed in the hands of states and non-state actors. Scenarios in which a military defence network can be remotely disabled or damaged, are plausible.^{6} Others, like using a cyber ‘worm' to shut down nuclear reactors^{7} or to incapacitate a whole country's health system^{8} are already a reality.\nOne of the most important aspects of the problem is how it is reflected on the jus ad bellum. This Latin expression refers to the legal rules establishing when states can use force in international relations.^{9} While early efforts date back at least to the 1919 DE Khan, ‘Territory and Boundaries' in B Fassbender and A Peters (eds), The Oxford Handbook of the History of International Law (OUP, 2012) 247.\n2. A Hern and S Gibbs, ‘What is WannaCry ransomware and why is it attacking global computers' (The Guardian, 12 May 2017), available at <https://www.theguardian.com/technology/2017/may/12/nhs-ransomware-cyber attack-what-is-wanacrypt0r-20> (accessed 6 April 2021).\n9. B Garner (ed), Black's Law Dictionary (10th edn, Thomson Reuters, 2014).\nThe Military Law and the Law of War Review, Vol. 59 No. 1\nCovenant of the League of Nations¹⁰ and the 1928 Pact of Paris,¹¹ the present cornerstone of the jus ad bellum matrix is enshrined in the United Nations Charter,¹² particularly in Article 2(4)¹³ and Chapter VII of the UN Charter.¹⁴\nIf law is to remain effective over time, it must be responsive to context. This axiom is no less true in cyberspace than in the kinetic environment. Technology is not immune to the legal system, nor is the legal system immune to technology.¹⁵ The enigma that is presented, is whether the norms of the jus ad bellum were affected in such a manner that they fail to apply in a cyber context.\nThis article is divided into two sections: The first section begins with an attempt to interpret Article 2(4) of the UN Charter and analyses the predominant approaches that exist regarding the elevation of a cyber attack to the use of force. It is subsequently divided into two sub-sections, the first, discussing cyber attacks causing, or reasonably likely to cause physical damage to property, loss of life, or injury of people, while the second subsection investigates attacks that cause severe disruption to national critical infrastructure. The last section of the second part occupies itself with cases of cyber attacks that do not reach the level of use of force and is further divided into two sub-sections, investigating cases where cyber attacks may be regarded as unlawful intervention to the internal affairs of a state, or as threats of force.\nConclusively, the present article excludes from its scope the issue of attribution of a cyber attack to a state, considering that this part of the process has been concluded and a cyber attack has been attributed to a certain state. It is also not concerned with issues of countermeasures as a potential response to a cyber attack, as those fall outside of the nexus of the jus ad bellum.\n# 2 CYBER OPERATIONS AND THE PROHIBITION OF THE THREAT OR USE OF FORCE\n## 2.1 Introduction\nArticle 2(4) of the UN Charter provides that '[a]ll Members [of the United Nations] shall refrain in their international relations from the threat or use of force against the\n10. This provision is generally considered to reflect customary international law and, at least with regard to its core, is also jus cogens.¹⁶ This prohibition is complemented¹⁷ by the customary international law¹⁸ rule which prohibits states from interfering in the internal affairs of other states,¹⁹ also encompassed in Article 2(7) of the UN Charter.²⁰\nWhen the UN Charter was drafted in 1945, computers were limited to performing basic mathematical functions. The internet did not appear until the 1990s, more than 45 years after the San Francisco Conference, when the UN Charter was drafted. However, discussions about cyber attacks have the potential to reignite debates over the scope of Article 2(4).²¹ Nevertheless, the discussions now would centre on whether cyber attacks are also included in the prohibition, instead of whether political and economic coercion are included.²²\nThe International Court of Justice enunciates in its Advisory Opinion regarding the Legality of the Threat or Use of Nuclear Weapons that Article 2(4) of the UN Charter prohibits the use of force regardless of the weapons employed.²³ In addition, states and international organizations have affirmed the applicability of the existing rules on the use of force in the cyber context. However, in the context of this article, only the second requirement, whether the cyber operation amounts to a threat or the use of force will be examined. The first part will address how a cyber operation can reach the threshold of the use of force, and the kind of force that is required. The second part will address cyber operations that fail to meet the threshold of the use of force and also the issues of cyber operations amounting to a threat of force.\n27. In case the usual means of interpretation are used, as encompassed in Article 31(1) of the Vienna Convention of the Law of Treaties⁴⁰ and in customary international law,⁴¹ the results are inconclusive.⁴² The definition of force according to Black’s Law Dictionary is ‘power, violence, or pressure directed against a person or thing’⁴³ making it broad enough to cover not only armed force but also political and economic coercion, which was clearly rejected by the drafters of the UN Charter.⁴⁴ The contextual path does not lead to a result either, as it is referenced in the Preamble,⁴⁵ in Articles 41⁴⁶ and 46⁴⁷ where it is receded by the adjective ‘armed’ and in Article 44 where the reference is clearly regarding armed force only.⁴⁸ Finally, subsequent UN documents, such as the 1970 Declaration on Friendly Relations,⁴⁹ the 1974 Declaration on the Definition of Aggression⁵⁰ and the 1987 Declaration on the Non-Use of Force⁵¹ support the view that Article 2(4) only refers to armed force.\nSir H Lauterpacht had concluded the issue in an impressive way by saying: ‘armed force is nothing other than an extreme form of intervention that, like economic and political coercion, is characterized by the intention of the coercing state to compel the victim state into doing or not doing something through a ‘dictatorial interference’ in its internal or external affairs’.⁵² Regarding the issue of whether cyber operations fall within the scope of Article 2(4), it ultimately depends on the approach taken regarding the understanding of armed force.⁵³ There are three predominant approaches on the matter:\nThe first one focuses on the instrument used to commit an act, that is the use of weapons has been used to distinguish between armed force and political or economic coercion.⁵⁴ Bombing computer servers or internet cables could meet the requirements of the use of force, for example.⁵⁵ This approach has been criticized as too centred to\n39. It argues that cyber operations reach the threshold of a use of force only when they aim at national critical infrastructures, whatever their effects on these infrastructures may be.^^ The criticism of this approach stems from the fact that is considered over-inclusive, and according to it cyber operations that clearly do not entail force, such as intelligence gathering may be regarded as the use of force, as long as they target national critical infrastructures.^^ In addition, it has been argued that there is no generally accepted definition for national critical infrastructure.^^\nThe final approach is based on the effects of the action^^ and it is usually called 'the effects doctrine', and has been characterized as the most proponent.^^ This approach, starting from the fact that armed force has direct destructive effects on property and people,^^ stipulates that if any cyber operation causes, or is likely to cause the damaging consequences that normally kinetic weapons do, then it is considered as the use of armed force.^^ This effects-based approach has also been embraced by the United States. In his speech at CYBERCOM, the State Department's Legal Advisor, Harold Koh, argued that 'if the physical consequences of a cyber attack work the kind of physical damage that dropping a bomb or firing a missile would, that cyber attack should equally be considered a use of force'.^^\nThis has also received its share of criticism, as the directness is not necessarily an inherent characteristic on the use of armed force,^^ a view also supported by the UN\n56. A case-by-case approach is supported⁶⁹ on the grounds of the absence of any primary source of international law, or the decision of an international tribunal, as well as the possibility that governments, until such a source is found, are likely to resort to the case-by-case approach individually and collectively.⁷⁰ Finally, it is argued that this model will be more appealing to governments worldwide.⁷¹\nConsidering the problems an open approach could create, and the possible risks of the creation of double standards, Michael Schmitt introduced a set of factors that influence states in their legal assessments of the use of force in and via cyberspace.⁷² He identified six criteria to serve this purpose: severity, immediacy, directness, invasiveness, measurability (of effects) and presumptive legitimacy.⁷³ Those criteria were revised in 2001 and ‘responsibility’ was added.⁷⁴ Finally, with the release of the Tallinn Manual and their inclusion in it, ‘responsibility’ was separated to ‘military character’ and ‘state involvement’ while the ‘presumptive legitimacy’ became ‘presumptive legality’,⁷⁵ classification that was also used for the revised version of the Manual.⁷⁶ According to Schmitt, these criteria are ‘not legal and merely factors that can be expected to influence states when making sure of force appraisals.’⁷⁷\nSchmitt’s criteria could not be of course, without criticism. ‘Directness’ for example cannot be considered as the inherent characteristic of the use of force, as already stated above. Regarding ‘invasiveness’, it must be mentioned that one common form of cyber attacks, ‘flood attacks’ are not intrusive as the attackers do not\n⁶⁶. UNGA Res 3314 (XXIX) (n 50).\n⁶⁷. Finally, the presumptive legality factor is based on what Judge Simma has defined as ‘an old, tired view of international law’,82 according to which ‘acts that are not forbidden are permitted’.83\nAmong the aforementioned theories, the effects-based approach is the one accepted by the majority and for a good reason. The instrument-based approach presents itself as too simplistic, and unsuitable for the rapidly changing environment of the cyberspace, resulting in not considering the uses of force cyber attacks which may as well qualify as such. On the other hand, the over-inclusive target-based approach, if adopted, presents the danger of characterizing as force incidents which do not fall within the threshold. Taking into account that cyber attacks are analysed under the scope of the use of force framework, the effects-based approach is the one that appears the most helpful84 as it is tantamount to requirements used in kinetic attacks as well.85\n## 2.2.1 Cyber attacks causing, or reasonably likely to cause physical damage to property, loss of life, or injury of persons\nCyber operations can have multiple effects86 on different fields. The primary effects are those on the computer or on the network that was attacked, the secondary effects on the infrastructure operated by said system and the tertiary effects on the people who were affected by the destruction or incapacitation of infrastructure.87 As Waxman notes, ‘modern society’s heavy reliance on interconnected information systems means that the indirect and secondary effects of cyber attacks may be much more consequential than the direct and immediate ones’.88 In addition, in the Nicaragua Judgement, the ICJ expressly recognized that intervention that uses armed force can occur directly or indirectly.89\n78. F Schreier, ‘On Cyberwarfare’ (Geneva Centre for Security Sector Governance, 20 March 2012) 51, available at <https: default=\"\" documents=\"\" files=\"\" publications=\"\" sites=\"\" www.dcaf.ch=\"\"> (accessed 6 April 2021).\n79. J Andress and S Winterfeld, Cyber Warfare (2nd edn, Syngress, 2013) 216.\n80. Ibid at 190.\n81. D Bainbridge, Introduction to Computer Law (5th edn, Longman Pub Group, 2004) 397.\n82. Nicaragua (n 13) para 205.\n© 2021 The Author\nJournal compilation © 2021 Edward Elgar Publishing Ltd</https:>\nThe prohibition of the threat or use of force in cyberspace 99\nA lot of voices agree that a cyber attack which causes or is reasonably likely to cause physical damage to property, loss of life or injury to people would fall under the prohibition contained in Article 2(4) of the UN Charter. According to the Tallinn Manual 2.0 ‘a cyber operation constitutes a use of force when its scale and effects are comparable to non-cyber operations rising to the level of a use of force’.⁹⁰ ‘Scale and effects’ is considered a shorthand term that captures the quantitative and qualitative factors to be analysed in the determination of whether a cyber operation reaches the threshold of Article 2(4).⁹¹ Harold Koh cites three examples of cyber operations amounting to the use of force: (1) operations that trigger a meltdown at a nuclear plant, (2) operations that open a dam above a populated area causing a destruction, and (3) operations that disable air traffic control resulting in airplane crashes.⁹² However, since there are no practical examples of this kind, three real examples, falling into the category of attacks that have caused physical damage to property, loss of life or injury to people and can be characterized as a use of force under Article 2(4) UN Charter, will be analysed.\nThere are no reported injuries or casualties caused by cyber attack, so far.⁹³ Maybe the first, almost legendary case of a cyber attack was the explosion of a Soviet gas pipeline in June 1982 which was caused by a logic bomb, inserted in the computer control system by the CIA.⁹⁴ Besides this dubious case,⁹⁵ the first known case of malicious software designed to produce actual material damage to physical property, and in particular by attacking the Supervisory Control and Data Acquisition (SCADA) system of a National critical infrastructure, was Stuxnet.⁹⁶ Exploiting until then unknown vulnerabilities, and with the ability to insert modifications into the system,⁹⁷ it forced changes to the centrifuges rotor at the Natanz uranium enrichment plant in Iran, inducing excessive vibrations or distortions that would damage the centrifuges.⁹⁸ As a result, Iran stopped using thousands of centrifuges at Natanz, even though it did not admit to it publicly.⁹⁹\nAnother known and massive cyber attack happened in Estonia in Spring 2007, when¹⁰⁰ botnets commanded zombie computers all over the world to overwhelm\n90. D McGuiness, ‘How a cyber attack transformed Estonia’ (*BBC News*, 27 April 2017), available at <https: 39655415=\"\" news=\"\" www.bbc.com=\"\"> (accessed 6 April 2021).\n© 2021 The Author\nJournal compilation © 2021 Edward Elgar Publishing Ltd</https:></www.nytimes.com></https:>\nThe Military Law and the Law of War Review, Vol. 59 No. 1\nEstonian websites with bogus traffic.¹⁰¹ Such attacks often cause mere inconvenience, but this attack had nearly life threatening consequences as the emergency lines to call an ambulance or a fire truck were out of service for an hour.¹⁰² Additionally, individual hackers wreaked targeted havoc. Banks, governmental services, media providers, and even the national emergency call number were affected.¹⁰³\nAfter examining the aforementioned cases, a question that arises is whether there is a minimum threshold of gravity that must be reached by the destructive consequences of a cyber operation for it to reach the threshold of the prohibition of Article 2(4). The opinions diverge.\nHarold Koh appears to distinguish between death or injury to people on the one hand, and damage to property on the other, arguing that ‘cyber activities that proximately result in death, injury or significant destruction that would be considered a use of force’.¹⁰⁴ Outside of the cyber context Olivier Corten argues that ‘there is a threshold below which the use of force in international relations, while it may be contrary to the certain rules of international law, cannot violate article 2(4)’.¹⁰⁵ In the same way, in its 2009 Report, the Independent International Fact-Finding Commission on the Conflict in Georgia found that ‘the prohibition of the use of force covers all physical force which surpasses a minimum threshold of intensity and only very small incidents lie below this threshold, for instance the targeted killing of single individuals, forcible abductions of individual persons, or the interception of a single aircraft’.¹⁰⁶\nThis opinion also seems to be subtly supported by the ICJ. In the 1998 Fisheries Jurisdiction judgment, while Spain argued that the forcible measures against the Estai should be considered as a violation of Article 2(4), the Court found that ‘the use of force authorized by the Canadian legislation and regulations falls within the ambit of what is commonly understood as enforcement of conservation and management measures’ and that ‘boarding, inspection, arrest and minimum use of force for those purposes are all contained within the concept of enforcement of conservation and management measures according to a ‘natural and reasonable’ interpretation of this concept’.¹⁰⁷\nA number of commentators disagree with the suggestion that Article 2(4) includes the factor of gravity when determining the cases of the use of force. Roberto Ago argues a wider inclusiveness of Article 2(4) suggesting that: ‘Article 2(4) prohibits any kind of conduct involving any assault whatsoever on the territorial sovereignty of\n101. In a series of national declarations regarding the applicability of international law in cyberspace, one can find the enunciation that the use of force is prohibited as a common element. Furthermore, when there is no agreed threshold of gravity for kinetic force, it surely begs the question how we can agree on such threshold in cases of cyber operations where different factors and characteristics of force are different. Moreover, the state practice regarding the use of force in a cyber context is not enough to deduce a gravity threshold. The essential threshold that characterizes the prohibition of the use of force is the application of armed force as a means of coercion, a feature affirmed by theory$^{122}$ and the $\\mathrm{ICJ}^{123}$ alike. In addition, this type of coercion provides the differentiation of the term from the notions of the prohibition of intervention$^{124}$ and the violation of sovereignty.$^{125}$ Besides that, no specific gravity threshold can be read into Article 2(4) nor be shown to exist in state practice.\n## 2.2.2 Cyber attacks severely disrupting the functioning of national critical infrastructures\nBesides cyber attacks employing capabilities that cause or are reasonably likely to cause material damage to property or people, and their possible equation to kinetic\n115. What about those cyber operations that are disruptive by nature and they render ineffective or unusable infrastructures without physically damaging them? Do they reach the threshold of the prohibition of Article 2(4)?\nIt can be considered plausible that those types of disruptive cyber operations may amount to the use of force, if the disruption caused is significant enough to affect state security.126 The Unites States of America agrees with that particular notion, as it is evidenced by its 1999 Department of Defense's Assessment of International Legal Issues in Information Operations, where it is stated that ‘corrupting the data in a nation's computerized systems for managing its military fuel, spare parts, transportation, troop mobilization, or medical supplies, therefore seriously interfering with its ability to conduct military operations', might be treated as the use of force.127\nAccording to the aforementioned phrase, we can also consider as uses of force, cyber operations that aim at the taking control of network weapons or weapon systems, such as the virus that infected US drones' cockpits.128 In addition, cyber attacks that severely disrupt other national critical infrastructures such as public health,129 transportation,130 food and water supply,131 will most likely result in damage to property or people and thus may be considered as uses of force.\nBefore proceeding further, it becomes essential to define the term ‘national critical infrastructure'. The problem is that there is no general agreement on what infrastructures are considered ‘critical': the UN General Assembly recognized that ‘each country will determine its own critical information infrastructures'.132 The 2010 US Joint Terminology for Cyberspace Operations defines ‘critical infrastructure' as ‘systems and assets, whether physical or virtual, so vital that the incapacity or destruction of such may have a debilitating impact on the security, economy, public health or safety, environment, or any combination of these matters, across any Federal, state, regional, territorial, or local jurisdiction'.133 The 2011 International Strategy for Cyberspace emphasizes the need to protect in particular the national\n126. According to an opinion, ‘a flexible interpretation of Article 2(4) according to the evolution of weaponry and the logic behind this provision does not prevent a broadening of its prohibition in order to incorporate new uses of force’.¹⁴⁵ Indeed, as has been observed, ‘if law is to remain effective over time, it must be responsive to context and this is no less true in cyberspace than in the kinetic environment’.¹⁴⁶ The concept of evolutionary interpretation¹⁴⁷ of treaties’ generic terms,¹⁴⁸ which are capable of accommodating change and development over time,¹⁴⁹ is a known method, also employed by the ICJ.¹⁵⁰\nFollowing the way generic terms have been interpreted by the ICJ, the same can be applied to the term ‘force’. The supporters of this idea argue that due to the increasing over-dependency of states on computer systems and networks to provide critical services for society, if we focus only on the direct destructive implications of cyber attacks in individuals and property, we are being reductive.¹⁵¹ Melzer remarks how the kinetic equivalence doctrine, considering as a use of force only those cyber operations that cause material damage comparable to kinetic weapons, is too restrictive.¹⁵²\n142. N Melzer, Cyberwarfare and International Law (UNIDIR, 2011) 14, available at <http: files=\"\" publications=\"\" unidir.org=\"\" www.unidir.org=\"\"> (accessed 6 April 2021).\n© 2021 The Author\nJournal compilation © 2021 Edward Elgar Publishing Ltd</http:>\nThe Military Law and the Law of War Review, Vol. 59 No. 1\nSimilarly, Tsagourias emphasizes that ‘a cyber attack on critical state infrastructure which paralyses or massively disrupts the apparatus of the state should be equated to an armed attack, even if it does not cause any immediate human injury or material damage’.¹⁵³\nAn ‘interpretive reorientation’¹⁵⁴ of Article 2(4) that also includes in the scope of the provision cyber attacks causing serious disruption of essential services without destroying infrastructures would also reflect the trend in modern warfare to favour incapacitation to destruction.¹⁵⁵ For instance, in the 1999 Operation Allied Force against Yugoslavia, NATO targeted switching stations instead of generation stations to enable fast repair after the conflict and used carbon-graphite filaments in such a way as to cause only temporary incapacitation of electricity.¹⁵⁶ The Rules of Engagement distributed to the US Military Forces in Operation Iraqi Freedom also provided that attacks at the enemy infrastructure, lines of communication and economic objects should be aimed at disabling and disrupting them, avoiding destruction whenever possible.¹⁵⁷\nThis idea is also supported by a portion of state practice. Mali has for instance claimed that ‘the use of an information weapon could be interpreted as an act of aggression if the victim state has reasons to believe that the attack was carried out by the armed forces of another state and was aimed at disrupting … economic capacity, or violating the state’s sovereignty over a particular territory’.¹⁵⁸ It has also been declared by a senior Russian military officer that ‘the use of Information Warfare against Russia or its armed forces will categorically not be considered a non-military phase of a conflict whether there were casualties or not’.¹⁵⁹ Finally, the US have given examples where they have stated that a cyber attack against its banking and financial systems,¹⁶⁰ or that an attack having a severe economic impact on the United States,¹⁶¹ shall be considered as the use of force.\nAn objection has been raised sometimes, that a cyber attack shutting down economic targets such as the stock exchange and disrupting financial markets amounts to economic coercion and not to armed force.¹⁶² This may not be considered correct for two reasons: First, general economic coercion does not have a specific target,¹⁶³\n¹⁵³. N Tsagourias, ‘Cyber Attacks, Self-defence and the Problem of Attribution’ (2012) 17 Journal Conflict &amp; Security Law 229, 231.\n¹⁵⁴. Waxman (n 21) 437.\n¹⁵⁵. S Oeter, ‘Methods and Means of Combat’ in D Fleck (ed), The Handbook of International Humanitarian Law (2nd edn, OUP, 2008) 129–30.\n¹⁵⁶. J Steiger, ‘Civilian Objects’ (2011) MPEPIL, para 14.\n¹⁵⁷. ‘Off target. The conduct of the war and civilian casualties in Iraq’ (Human Rights Watch, 11 December 2003) 138–39, available at <https: 2003=\"\" reports=\"\" us1203=\"\" usaa1203.pdf=\"\" www.hrw.org=\"\"> (accessed 6 April 2021).\n¹⁵⁸. UNGA (n 24) 8.\n¹⁵⁹. V Antolin-Jenkins, ‘Defining the Parameters of Cyberwar Operations: Looking for Law in all the Wrong Places?’ (2005) 51 Naval Law Review 132, 166.\n¹⁶⁰. US Department of Defense (n 64) 18.\n¹⁶¹. ‘Cyberspace Policy Report. A Report to Congress Pursuant to the National Defense Authorization Act for Fiscal Year 2011’ (US Department of Defence, 2011) 4, available at <https: cs=\"\" b8424=\"\" b424.docs=\"\" cyber-059.pdf=\"\" nsaebb=\"\" nsaebb=\"\" nsaebb.nsae=\"\"> (accessed 6 April 2021).\n¹⁶². E Wilmshurst, ‘The Chatham House Principles of International Law on the Use of Force in Self-Defence’ (2006) 55 International and Comparative Law Quarterly 963, 965.\n¹⁶³. B Carter, ‘Economic Coercion’ (2009) MPEPIL, para 2.\n© 2021 The Author\nJournal compilation © 2021 Edward Elgar Publishing Ltd</https:></https:>\nThe prohibition of the threat or use of force in cyberspace 107\nwhile cyber attacks do have a specific target.¹⁶⁴ Secondly, while economic coercion employs the economy as the means of pressure,¹⁶⁵ that is, an embargo; a cyber attack incapacitates the financial market or cripples state banking system, the economy is the target rather than the means.¹⁶⁶\nThus, if the stock exchange of a country was to be bombed kinetically and the markets disrupted as a consequence, it will surely be perceived as the use of force and not as an economic coercion. The fact that the same would not be considered for the closing of the stock exchange due to a massive cyber attack, is absurd.¹⁶⁷ If, however, the disruption caused is not severe, ‘the cyber attack may be less likely to be regarded as the use of force than a kinetic attack with the same (temporary) economic effect, simply because the lack of physical destruction would reduce the scale of the damage caused’.¹⁶⁸\nIt must be emphasized that only cyber attacks that go beyond mere inconvenience and significantly disrupt the functioning of critical infrastructure can potentially fall under the scope of Article 2(4).¹⁶⁹ It is only in these cases that the effects of disruption can be equated to those destruction caused by traditional armed force. A cyber attack lasting weeks and shutting down the national grid, leaving millions of people without electricity and generally causing havoc to the city, is likely to be treated as the use of force, regardless of any physical damage ensuing.¹⁷⁰ On the other hand, a cyber attack that shuts down the network of a particular university, will not be considered as the use of force, even if the disruption last for several weeks.\nThe conversation can proceed endlessly and at some point, it becomes devoid of its meaning. The fear that exists about the potential of a disruptive cyber operation being considered an armed attack, and thus giving rise to a right of self-defence is not valid.¹⁷¹ The only legal consequence of qualifying seriously disruptive cyber operations as the use of force, is that they cannot be taken as a counter-measure, a welcome result in today’s digitally reliant societies.¹⁷² Finally, the inclusion of financial institutions among national critical infrastructures, and the classification of a cyber attack that severely disrupts them as a cyber attack, is in accord with the effects\n¹⁶⁴. ILC, Responsibility of States for Internationally Wrongful Acts with commentaries UN Doc A/56/10 (2001) (State Responsibility Commentary), Art 50(1)(a).\n© 2021 The Author\nJournal compilation © 2021 Edward Elgar Publishing Ltd</http:>\nThe Military Law and the Law of War Review, Vol. 59 No. 1\ndoctrine and also recognizes the vital part that those institutions play in the global economy.\n# 3 CYBER OPERATIONS NOT REACHING THE THRESHOLD OF ARTICLE 2(4) UN CHARTER\n## 3.1 Cyber operations amounting to an unlawful intervention to the internal affairs of a state\nIn a case when a cyber attack is not disruptive enough or if it targets non-critical infrastructures and does not amount to a violation of Article 2(4), this does not render it legal, according to international law. If such an attack is attributed to a state, it can amount to a violation of the principle of non-intervention in the internal affairs of another state.^^\nThe customary principle^^ of non-intervention derives primarily from the international law notions of sovereignty and territory.^^ Non-intervention not only permits states to operate free from outside interference by other states, but also reinforces the notion of sovereign equality among states.^^ The common denominator of all unlawful interventions, and the way to differentiate it by the violation of sovereignty,^^ is, according to the ICJ, the element of coercion.^^ Thus, to qualify as an unlawful intervention, interference must be forcible, dictatorial or otherwise coercive and in effect depriving the state of control over the matter in question.^^ What distinguishes intervention from a violation of Article 2(4) is that it can occur 'with or without armed force'.^^\nThe principle of non-intervention has been incorporated in a multitude of international agreements.^^ In addition, it appears in a series of UN Documents. The 1965 UN General Assembly Declaration on the Inadmissibility of Intervention in the Domestic Affairs of States and the Protection of their Independence and Sovereignty,^^ the 1970 Declaration on Friendly Relations,^^ the 1976 Declaration on Non-interference^^ and the 1981 General Assembly Declaration on the Inadmissibility of Intervention and Interference in the Internal Affairs of States^^ all condemn all\n173. UN Charter, Art 2(7); Schmitt et al. (n 76) 312.\n174. Nicaragua (n 13) para 205.\n175. Armed Activities on the Territory of the Congo (DRC v. Uganda), Merits, Judgment  ICJ Rep 168, paras 161-63.\n176. Corfu Channel (United Kingdom v. Albania), Merits, Judgment  ICJ Rep 35.\n177. Nicaragua (n 13) para 205; Armed Activities on the Territory of the Congo (n 175) para 164.\n179. A Cassese, International Law (OUP, 2001) 100; Shaw (n 18) 874.\n180. Nicaragua (n 13) para 206.\n181. UNGA Res 36/103 (9 December 1981).\n© 2021 The Author\nJournal compilation © 2021 Edward Elgar Publishing Ltd\nThe prohibition of the threat or use of force in cyberspace 109\nforms of intervention and they use similarly broad language, able to include cyber attacks below the level of the use of force.¹⁸⁶\nMoreover, state practice seems to agree with this particular way of thinking. In 1989 a bilateral treaty was signed between the US and the Soviet Union on the prevention of dangerous military activities which *inter alia* prohibits ‘interfering with command and control networks in a manner which could cause harm to personnel or damage to equipment of the armed forces of the other Party’.¹⁸⁷ Article I(9) of the treaty in particular, defines ‘interference with command and control networks’ as ‘actions that hamper, interrupt or limit the operation of the signals and information transmission means and systems providing for the control of personnel and equipment of the armed forces of a Party’.¹⁸⁸\nA cyber attack can also amount to unlawful intervention in cases other than low level disruption. Defacing websites in order to cause civil strife in a state, incitement to violence through cyber means¹⁸⁹ or sending emails to voters before elections in another state to influence them, may be considered prime examples of unlawful intervention.¹⁹⁰ A good example would be the cyber attacks in September 2012 in Azerbaijan, conducted by the ‘Armenian Cyber Army’. Said attacks were supposedly under the ‘direction and control’ of Armenia and were ‘aimed at glorifying terrorists and insulting their victims, as well as at advocating, promoting and inciting ethnically and religiously motivated hatred, discrimination and violence’.¹⁹¹ As it has been observed, if a broadcast ‘is deliberately false and intended to produce dissent or encourage insurgents, the non-intervention principle is likely to be breached’.¹⁹²\nAn issue that must be addressed when discussing intervention in a cyber context, is the issue of propaganda. Propaganda has been defined as a communication ‘to shape attitudes and behaviour … for political purposes’.¹⁹³ To constitute an intervention, propaganda must involve not merely persuasion but rather coercion. To discern coercive propaganda, publicists have asked ‘is the audience’s choice of alternatives severely restricted as a result of the use of the instrument?’¹⁹⁴ It might be said with respect to cyber propaganda that legitimacy depends less on means than ‘upon its outcome, actual or expected’.¹⁹⁵ Acts that amount merely to persuasion remain legitimate.¹⁹⁶\n¹⁸⁶. S Watts, ‘Low-Intensity Cyber Operations and the Principle of Non-Intervention’ in J David Ohlin et al. (eds), *Cyber War: Law and Ethics for Virtual Conflicts* (OUP, 2015) 249, 253.\n¹⁸⁷. Similarly, a state's offer of incentives or favourable treatment to induce a course of action by another state would not constitute intervention. Only where propaganda could be said to constitute significant support for coercion on a level commensurate with the logistical and financial support recognized by the Nicaragua judgment, could cyber propaganda amount to a violation of the principle of non-intervention.^^\nAn issue that has brought the principle of non-intervention and its application in the cyber context to the forefront, is the disruption of electoral processes. Both the 2016 US presidential election and the 2017 French presidential election have been victims of extended cyber operations.^^ Specifically, in the case of the US election the governing body of the United States Democratic Party, the Democratic National Committee (DNC) was targeted by a series of cyber attacks resulting in data and email theft^^ and the attack was attributed to the Russian Federation which denied it.^^ In the case of the French Presidential election the party of the now President of France, Emmanuel Macron, was hacked and gigabytes of emails and documents were published online.^^ Both of the aforementioned acts have been discussed to be an intervention in the internal affairs of the state, as they interfere with an issue in the domain reserve of the state, the elections.^^\nIn the twentieth century the notion of 'intervention' was broadened as a consequence of the increased cooperation among states allowing subtler ways of interference than the use of force.^^ Thus, a further broadening of the notion to include cyber operations is now dictated by the interconnectivity of networks and the reliance of the modern society in computer systems.\nSaid broadening may also be supported by the growing record of state practice. First, states have the tendency not to engage openly in cyber attacks, but to hide their responsibility by camouflaging their attacks through technical means.^^ As Thomas\n197. Watts (n 186) 253.\n198. In other words, the fact that states attempt to hide their cyber attack is a possible concern that these attacks may constitute unlawful uses of force. Secondly, when states admit that they have been victims of cyber attack, they and their allies usually denounce and condemn the attacks.\nNevertheless, the opinio juris of states has also evolved to engage the non-intervention principle in the context of cyber operations. Specifically, in the 2015 Report of the Group of Governmental Experts on Developments in the Field of Information and Telecommunications in the Context of International Security, it has been specifically mentioned that the principle of non-intervention must be observed in the use of ICTs by States. Furthermore, in a number of subsequent documents, many individual states have reiterated their opinion that cyber operations may violate the principle of non-intervention.\n### 3.2 A cyber threat of force?\nArticle 2(4) of the UN Charter sets besides the prohibition of the use of force, the prohibition of the use of threat to use force as well. A threat of force, according to Ian Brownlie, is an ‘express or implied promise' to ‘resort to force conditional on non-acceptance of certain demands' of the threatening government. The main element of such a threat of force is the coercive intent of the threat. It has been commented that the importance of those two prohibitions is exactly the same with no differences whatsoever. However, it is a fact that the prohibition of the threat to use force has been examined quite less than the prohibition of the actual use of force. The question that is presented is simple: Can a cyber operation amount to a threat to use force?\n205. TM Franck, ‘Legitimacy After Kosovo and Iraq' in V Cmid-Grotid and M Matulovic (eds), International Law and The Use Of Force At The Turn Of Centuries: Essays In Honour Of V.D. Degan (Rijeka, 2005) 73.\n206. UN Charter, Art 2(4).\n210. I Brownlie, International Law and the Use of Force by States (OUP, 1963) 364.\n211. Dörr and Randelzhofer (n 22) 216.\n212. Dissenting Opinion, Judge Weeramantry in Legality of the Threat or Use of Nuclear Weapons (Advisory Opinion)  ICJ Rep 226, 526.\n213. R Sadurska, ‘Threats of Force' (1988) 82 AJIL 239; M Roscini, ‘Threats of Armed Force and Contemporary International Law' (2007) 54 Netherlands International Law Review 229; N Stürchler, The Threat of Force in International Law (CUP, 2009); D Kritsiotis, ‘Close Encounters of a Sovereign Kind' (2009) 20 EJIL 299.\nThe Military Law and the Law of War Review, Vol. 59 No. 1\nA threat to use force may be communicated orally, in communications or documents, included in a treaty, or implied by certain military and political actions.²¹⁴ According to the ICJ on the ‘Legality of the Threat or Use of Nuclear Weapons’ Advisory Opinion, if the envisaged use of force is in itself unlawful, the stated readiness to use it would be an unlawful threat of force.²¹⁵\nOne can consider it pointless to continue discussing such a specialized form of the threat of force, when the notion itself has been this under researched.²¹⁶ Furthermore, the threat of force is a notion which states hesitate to invoke, as they prefer a situation where actions, which would be considered as threats of force, are not considered unlawful and are tolerated.²¹⁷ Moreover, the analysis of the Tallinn Manual, regarding the threat to use force in a cyber context, appears minimal. Besides mentioning the already known prerequisites of a threat to force, it ponders the questions of evaluation of the capacity to conduct a cyber threat of force, and the scenario of a state threatening to use force, while not meaning to do so.²¹⁸\nNevertheless, instances where states complain about unlawful threats of force are not as rare as one might think. Even in the context of cyberspace, threats of force may be manifested as explicit or they may result implicitly from certain conduct.²¹⁹ For example, the demonstration of force for the purpose of exercising political pressure could amount to a threat of force under the requirements of Article 2(4).²²⁰ A possible example would be cyber warfare simulations and other military exercises.²²¹\nMoreover, another common, albeit not much discussed form in which threats of force are expressed in cyberspace, are in the form of *ultimata*. Such threatening declarations have been long considered as violating the rule prohibiting threats of force in international law²²² and it ought to be seriously examined whether this should not be the case in the context of cyberspace. Moreover, recent events have evinced that simple Twitter statements can be interpreted as threats of force, when delivered by the relevant figure.²²³\nUltimately, it is submitted that even though elusive as a concept, the threat of force should not be outright excluded in the cyber context. On the contrary, due to the ease of means that cyberspace offers, it appears that the notion has a greater degree of normative space to evolve than in the traditional kinetic context. Even though the application of international law in cyberspace has been tenuous and not without its obstacles, the fact that a threat of force is communicated through cyberspace should be regarded as exactly this: a threat. The internet is just the form of communication employed, which has nothing to do with the actual message.²²⁴\n214. Sadurska ibid at 242–43.\n215. *Nuclear Weapons Advisory Opinion* (n 23) para 47.\n216. Delrue (n 202) 315; Roscini (n 53) 67–68.\n© 2021 The Author\nJournal compilation © 2021 Edward Elgar Publishing Ltd\nThe prohibition of the threat or use of force in cyberspace 113\n# 4 CONCLUSION\nThe provisions of the UN Charter regarding the use of force between states, seem to also apply to cyber attacks, even though their adoption was decades before the appearance of cyberspace. The flexibility of the provision, as well as the interpretative guidance given by theory and jurisprudence, have contributed to the potential invocation of Article 2(4) of the UN Charter, even in cases of cyber attacks.\nIt has been argued that a cyber attack may be considered as the use of armed force, when its effects are similar to those of a kinetic attack. This occurs in cases when cyber attacks result in loss of life, injury of people or physical damage to property, without imposing any certain threshold, regarding the gravity of the use of force. In addition, cases of severe disruption of essential services or infrastructures, caused by cyber operations, may be considered as uses of force, even though no destruction or material damage to property occurred. The increasing digitalization of today's societies has made it possible to cause considerable harm to states through non-destructive means and a modern interpretation and application of Article 2(4) should have taken this into account.\nIn conclusion, cyber attacks conducted by states, that fail to reach the level of the use of force, may be considered a violation of the principle of non-intervention – if they are considered ‘a manifestation of a policy of force’ and if a coercive intent is recognized in that attempt. Finally, the notion of a cyber threat of force is dismissed, due to the ambiguity of the notion, as well as the reluctance of states to even invoke it.\n© 2021 The Author\nJournal compilation © 2021 Edward Elgar Publishing Ltd"

          "intext_citation": "(Brill, 2014)",
          "preceding_text": "⁶⁹. Y Radziwill, Cyber Attacks and the Exploitable Imperfection of International Law",
          "footnote": "126. Y Radziwill, Cyber Attacks and the Exploitable Imperfection of International Law (Brill, 2014) 132."

        {
          "index": "nato ccdcoe|2012",
          "intext_citation": "(NATO CCDCOE, 2012)",
          "preceding_text": "(eds), 4th International Conference on Cyber Conflict",
          "footnote": "Roscini (n 53) 48. © 2021 The Author Journal compilation © 2021 Edward Elgar Publishing Ltd</https:> The prohibition of the threat or use of force in cyberspace 97 General Assembly Declaration on the Definition of Aggression⁶⁶ as well as the ICJ.⁶⁷ In addition, this approach, according to some critics, leaves out the areas of economic sanctions and cyber espionage.⁶⁸ Beside the aforementioned three approaches, there are certain others that have been developed by international legal theory. A case-by-case approach is supported⁶⁹ on the grounds of the absence of any primary source of international law, or the decision of an international tribunal, as well as the possibility that governments, until such a source is found, are likely to resort to the case-by-case approach individually and collectively.⁷⁰ Finally, it is argued that this model will be more appealing to governments worldwide.⁷¹ Considering the problems an open approach could create, and the possible risks of the creation of double standards, Michael Schmitt introduced a set of factors that influence states in their legal assessments of the use of force in and via cyberspace.⁷² He identified six criteria to serve this purpose: severity, immediacy, directness, invasiveness, measurability (of effects) and presumptive legitimacy.⁷³ Those criteria were revised in 2001 and ‘responsibility’ was added.⁷⁴ Finally, with the release of the Tallinn Manual and their inclusion in it, ‘responsibility’ was separated to ‘military character’ and ‘state involvement’ while the ‘presumptive legitimacy’ became ‘presumptive legality’,⁷⁵ classification that was also used for the revised version of the Manual.⁷⁶ According to Schmitt, these criteria are ‘not legal and merely factors that can be expected to influence states when making sure of force appraisals.’⁷⁷ Schmitt’s criteria could not be of course, without criticism. ‘Directness’ for example cannot be considered as the inherent characteristic of the use of force, as already stated above. Regarding ‘invasiveness’, it must be mentioned that one common form of cyber attacks, ‘flood attacks’ are not intrusive as the attackers do not ⁶⁶. UNGA Res 3314 (XXIX) (n 50). ⁶⁷. Nicaragua (n 13) para 228. ⁶⁸. T Kliem, ‘You Can’t Cyber in Here, this is the War Room! A Rejection of the Effects Doctrine on Cyberwar and the Use of Force in International Law’ (2017) 4 Journal on the Use of Force and International Law 344, 351. ⁶⁹. Y Radziwill, Cyber Attacks and the Exploitable Imperfection of International Law (Brill, 2014) 139. ⁷⁰. D Tubbs et al., ‘Technology and Law: The Evolution of Digital Warfare’ (2002) 76 International Law Studies 15. ⁷¹. Waxman (n 21) 454. ⁷². M Schmitt, ‘Cyber Operations and the Jus Ad Bellum Revisited’ (2011) 56 Villanova Law Review 569, 575; M Schmitt, ‘The ‘Use of Force’ in Cyberspace: A Reply to Dr Ziolkowski’ in C Czosseck et al. (eds), 4th International Conference on Cyber Conflict (NATO CCDCOE, 2012) 314–17. ⁷³. M Schmitt, ‘Computer Network Attack and the Use of Force in International Law: Thoughts on a Normative Framework’ (1999) 37 Columbia Journal of Transnational Law 885, 914–15. ⁷⁴. M Schmitt, ‘Computer Network Attack: The Normative Software’ (2001) 4 Yearbook of International Humanitarian Law 53, 65. ⁷⁵. M Schmitt et al., Tallinn Manual on the International Law Applicable to Cyber Warfare (CUP, 2013) 48–51. ⁷⁶. M Schmitt et al., Tallinn Manual 2.0 on the International Law Applicable to Cyber Operations (CUP, 2017) 334–36. ⁷⁷. M Schmitt, ‘The “Use of Force” in Cyberspace: A Reply to Dr Ziolkowski’ in C Czosseck, K Ziolkowski and R Ottis (eds), 4th International Conference on Cyber Conflicts (NATO CCD COE, 2012) 314. © 2021 The Author Journal compilation © 2021 Edward Elgar Publishing Ltd The Military Law and the Law of War Review, Vol. 59 No. 1 gain access into the system, and they simply bar the entryways to the system.78 The damage caused is the flood of requests that the botnets79 or the individual computers send to the target system.80 Concerning ‘immediacy’, the existence of logic bombs, malicious programs designed to produce effects only at the certain time or when certain circumstances occur,81 indicate that there are cases of cyber operations where immediacy does not exist. Finally, the presumptive legality factor is based on what Judge Simma has defined as ‘an old, tired view of international law’,82 according to which ‘acts that are not forbidden are permitted’.83 Among the aforementioned theories, the effects-based approach is the one accepted by the majority and for a good reason. The instrument-based approach presents itself as too simplistic, and unsuitable for the rapidly changing environment of the cyberspace, resulting in not considering the uses of force cyber attacks which may as well qualify as such. On the other hand, the over-inclusive target-based approach, if adopted, presents the danger of characterizing as force incidents which do not fall within the threshold. Taking into account that cyber attacks are analysed under the scope of the use of force framework, the effects-based approach is the one that appears the most helpful84 as it is tantamount to requirements used in kinetic attacks as well.85 ## 2.2.1 Cyber attacks causing, or reasonably likely to cause physical damage to property, loss of life, or injury of persons Cyber operations can have multiple effects86 on different fields. The primary effects are those on the computer or on the network that was attacked, the secondary effects on the infrastructure operated by said system and the tertiary effects on the people who were affected by the destruction or incapacitation of infrastructure.87 As Waxman notes, ‘modern society’s heavy reliance on interconnected information systems means that the indirect and secondary effects of cyber attacks may be much more consequential than the direct and immediate ones’.88 In addition, in the Nicaragua Judgement, the ICJ expressly recognized that intervention that uses armed force can occur directly or indirectly.89"

M Schmitt et al., Tallinn Manual 2.0 on the International Law Applicable to Cyber Operations (CUP, 2017) 334–36. ⁷⁷. M Schmitt, ‘The “Use of Force” in Cyberspace: A Reply to Dr Ziolkowski’ in C Czosseck, K Ziolkowski and R Ottis (eds), 4th International Conference on Cyber Conflicts (NATO CCD COE, 2012) 314. © 2021 The Author Journal compilation © 2021 Edward Elgar Publishing Ltd The Military Law and the Law of War Review, Vol. 59 No. 1 gain access into the system, and they simply bar the entryways to the system.78 The damage caused is the flood of requests that the botnets79 or the individual computers send to the target system.80 Concerning ‘immediacy’, the existence of logic bombs, malicious programs designed to produce effects only at the certain time or when certain circumstances occur,81 indicate that there are cases of cyber operations where immediacy does not exist. Finally, the presumptive legality factor is based on what Judge Simma has defined as ‘an old, tired view of international law’,82 according to which ‘acts that are not forbidden are permitted’.83 Among the aforementioned theories, the effects-based approach is the one accepted by the majority and for a good reason. The instrument-based approach presents itself as too simplistic, and unsuitable for the rapidly changing environment of the cyberspace, resulting in not considering the uses of force cyber attacks which may as well qualify as such. On the other hand, the over-inclusive target-based approach, if adopted, presents the danger of characterizing as force incidents which do not fall within the threshold. Taking into account that cyber attacks are analysed under the scope of the use of force framework, the effects-based approach is the one that appears the most helpful84 as it is tantamount to requirements used in kinetic attacks as well.85 ## 2.2.1 Cyber attacks causing, or reasonably likely to cause physical damage to property, loss of life, or injury of persons Cyber operations can have multiple effects86 on different fields. The primary effects are those on the computer or on the network that was attacked, the secondary effects on the infrastructure operated by said system and the tertiary effects on the people who were affected by the destruction or incapacitation of infrastructure.87 As Waxman notes, ‘modern society’s heavy reliance on interconnected information systems means that the indirect and secondary effects of cyber attacks may be much more consequential than the direct and immediate ones’.88 In addition, in the Nicaragua Judgement, the ICJ expressly recognized that intervention that uses armed force can occur directly or indirectly.89"
